    var xhr = new XMLHttpRequest();

    xhr.open('GET', 'Sample.txt', true);

    xhr.onload = function() {
        if (xhr.status === 200) {
            document.getElementById('response').style.display = 'block';
            document.getElementById('response').textContent = xhr.responseText;
        } else {
            document.getElementById('response').textContent = 'Error loading the file!';
        }
    };

    xhr.onerror = function() {
        document.getElementById('response').textContent = 'Request failed. Please try again later.';
    };

    xhr.send();
});

Shift-in Sequence 1011:

Time = 25: Parallel Out becomes 0001, reflecting the shifted-in bit. Serial Out remains 0.

Holding State:

Time = 75: Serial In is 1, which fills the register to 1111.
Reset Again:

End of Simulation:

Product Owner (with a technical focus)
Responsibilities: Works closely with developers and stakeholders to define product requirements and prioritize tasks. A technical product owner combines business goals with technical feasibility, ensuring smooth communication between product management and engineering teams.
Skills: Product management, understanding of software development, stakeholder management, and agile methodologies.

Engineering Director
Responsibilities: Oversees multiple engineering teams or an entire engineering department, setting strategic goals and ensuring the technical direction aligns with business objectives. They are responsible for budgeting, team growth, and resource allocation.
Skills: Leadership, strategic planning, budget management, and cross-functional collaboration.

UI/UX Lead
Responsibilities: Leads the design and user experience strategy, ensuring that the interface is intuitive, visually appealing, and aligned with the brand's objectives. They work closely with both the design team and frontend developers to ensure seamless implementation of designs.
Skills: Strong UI/UX design skills, knowledge of frontend technologies, collaboration with design and development teams, and user research.

Web Development Lead
Responsibilities: Leads a team of developers focused on web applications, managing both frontend and backend development to ensure high-quality user experiences. They may be responsible for driving the entire lifecycle of frontend development, from wireframes to deployment.
Skills: Full-stack knowledge with a focus on frontend technologies, leadership, mentoring, and project management.

19/02/25, 10:08 - Sohil Nigga (Drug Dealer Who Can't Eat Drugs): #include <stdio.h>
#include <limits.h>
#include <stdbool.h>
#include <string.h>

#define MAX_N 10

int n;
int cost[MAX_N][MAX_N];
bool vis[MAX_N];
int min_cost = INT_MAX;
int best_path[MAX_N + 1];
int cur_path[MAX_N + 1];

void tsp(int lvl, int cur_cost) {
    if (lvl == n) {
        if (cost[cur_path[lvl - 1]][0] != -1) {
            int total_cost = cur_cost + cost[cur_path[lvl - 1]][0];
            if (total_cost < min_cost) {
                min_cost = total_cost;
                memcpy(best_path, cur_path, sizeof(int) * n);
                best_path[n] = 0;
            }
        }
        return;
    }
    
    for (int i = 1; i < n; i++) {
        if (!vis[i] && cost[cur_path[lvl - 1]][i] != -1) {
            vis[i] = true;
            cur_path[lvl] = i;
            tsp(lvl + 1, cur_cost + cost[cur_path[lvl - 1]][i]);
            vis[i] = false;
        }
    }
}

void solve() {
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        bool has_path = false;
        for (int j = 0; j < n; j++) {
            if (i != j && cost[i][j] != -1) {
                has_path = true;
                break;
            }
        }
        if (!has_path) {
            printf("Path does not exist\n");
            return;
        }
    }
    
    memset(vis, false, sizeof(vis));
    cur_path[0] = 0;
    vis[0] = true;
    tsp(1, 0);
    
    if (min_cost == INT_MAX) {
        printf("Path does not exist\n");
    } else {
        printf("Minimum Cost: %d\nOptimal Path: ", min_cost);
        for (int i = 0; i <= n; i++) {
            printf("%d ", best_path[i]);
        }
        printf("\n");
    }
}

int main() {
    scanf("%d", &n);
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        for (int j = 0; j < n; j++) {
            scanf("%d", &cost[i][j]);
        }
    }
    solve();
    return 0;
}

05/03/25, 15:24 - Sohil Nigga (Drug Dealer Who Can't Eat Drugs): #QF1_PES1IG23AM310_PES1UG23AM324.py

import numpy as np
import scipy.linalg

def plu_factorization(A):
    P, L, U = scipy.linalg.lu(A)
    return P, L, U

def main():
    A = []
    while True:
        try:
            row = input().strip()
            if row:
                A.append(list(map(float, row.split())))
            else:
                break
        except EOFError:
            break
    
    if not A:
        print("Error: No input provided.")
        return
    
    A = np.array(A)
    
    if A.shape[0] != A.shape[1]:
        print("Error: Input must be a square matrix.")
        return
    
    P, L, U = plu_factorization(A)

    print("P =")
    for row in P:
        print(" ".join(f"{x:.1f}" for x in row))
    print("\nL =")
    for row in L:
        print(" ".join(f"{x:.1f}" for x in row))
    print("\nU =")
    for row in U:
        print(" ".join(f"{x:.1f}" for x in row))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()

20/03/25, 10:59 - Sohil Nigga (Drug Dealer Who Can't Eat Drugs): GNU nano 8.3                                                                                                     my_module.c                                                                                                              
#include <linux/module.h>
#include <linux/kernel.h>
#include <linux/init.h>
#include <linux/sched.h>
#include <linux/sched/signal.h>
#include <linux/slab.h>
#include <linux/kthread.h>
#include <linux/delay.h>

#define NUM_CHILDREN 3

static struct task_struct *task;

static int child_thread(void *data) {
    pid_t pid = current->pid;
    char *mem = kmalloc(1024, GFP_KERNEL);
    if (!mem) {
        pr_err("Failed to allocate memory for child %d\n", pid);
        return -ENOMEM;
    }

    pr_info("Child process created: PID=%d, Allocated Memory=%px\n", pid, mem);
    ssleep(2);

    kfree(mem);
    pr_info("Child process exiting: PID=%d, Freed Memory=%px\n", pid, mem);
    return 0;
}

static int __init my_module_init(void) {
    int i;
    pr_info("Kernel Module Loaded: Parent PID=%d\n", current->pid);

    for (i = 0; i < NUM_CHILDREN; i++) {
        task = kthread_run(child_thread, NULL, "child_thread_%d", i);
        if (IS_ERR(task)) {
            pr_err("Failed to create child thread\n");
        }
    }
    return 0;
}

static void __exit my_module_exit(void) {
    pr_info("Kernel Module Unloaded\n");
}

module_init(my_module_init);
module_exit(my_module_exit);

MODULE_LICENSE("GPL");
MODULE_AUTHOR("Sohil N S");
MODULE_DESCRIPTION("Linux Kernel Module for Creating Child Processes and Allocating Memory");

16/04/25, 15:19 - Sohil Nigga (Drug Dealer Who Can't Eat Drugs): import numpy as np
import sys

class DimensionalityReducer:
    def __init__(self):
        self.mean = None
        self.basis = None  # Principal components (rows)
        self.variance_explained = None

    def preprocess(self, X):
        """Center the data by subtracting mean of each feature."""
        if not isinstance(X, np.ndarray):
            X = np.array(X)
        self.mean = np.mean(X, axis=0)
        return X - self.mean

    def compute_key_directions(self, X_centered):
        """Compute principal components using SVD."""
        U, S, VT = np.linalg.svd(X_centered, full_matrices=False)
        self.basis = VT
        total_variance = np.sum(S**2)
        if total_variance == 0:
            self.variance_explained = np.zeros_like(S)
        else:
            self.variance_explained = (S**2) / total_variance

    def reduce_dimensions(self, X_centered, k):
        """Project data onto k principal components."""
        return X_centered @ self.basis[:k].T

    def reconstruct(self, X_reduced):
        """Reconstruct original data from reduced dimensions."""
        if X_reduced.size == 0:
            return np.tile(self.mean, (1, 1)) if self.mean.ndim == 2 else self.mean
        return X_reduced @ self.basis[:X_reduced.shape[1]] + self.mean

    def evaluate_error(self, X_original, X_reconstructed):
        """
        Compute reconstruction error using Frobenius norm.
        For matrices X and Y, Frobenius norm is sqrt(sum((X - Y)^2))
        """
        if isinstance(X_reconstructed, np.ndarray):
            X_reconstructed = np.tile(X_reconstructed, (len(X_original), 1))
        return np.sqrt(np.sum((X_original - X_reconstructed) ** 2))
    
def main():
    # Read input matrix from stdin
    A = []
    while True:
        try:
            row = input().strip()
            if row:
                A.append(list(map(float, row.split())))
            else:
                break 
        except EOFError:
            break
    A = np.array(A)
    
    reducer = DimensionalityReducer()
    
    # Step 1: Preprocess (center the data)
    try:
        X_centered = reducer.preprocess(A)
    except Exception as e:
        print("Error during preprocessing:", e)
        return
    
    print("Centered data:")
    print(X_centered)
    
    # Step 2: Compute key directions (SVD)
    try:
        reducer.compute_key_directions(X_centered)
    except Exception as e:
        print("Error during key directions computation:", e)
        return
    
    # Determine k for 95% variance
    cumulative_variance = np.cumsum(reducer.variance_explained)
    k = np.argmax(cumulative_variance >= 0.95) + 1
    if k == 0:  # Handle case where no component meets the threshold
        k = len(reducer.variance_explained)
    
    # Handle case when all variances are zero (e.g., constant features)
    if np.allclose(X_centered, 0):
        k = 0
    
    # Step 3: Reduce dimensions
    try:
        if k > 0:
            X_reduced = reducer.reduce_dimensions(X_centered, k)
        else:
            X_reduced = np.zeros((X_centered.shape[0], 0))
    except Exception as e:
        print("Error during dimensionality reduction:", e)
        return
    
    print("\nTop directions:")
    if k > 0:
        for direction in reducer.basis[:k]:
            print(" ".join(f"{x:.2f}" for x in direction))
    else:
        print("No directions (all features are constant)")
    
    print("\nReduced data:")
    if X_reduced.size > 0:
        for row in X_reduced:
            print(" ".join(f"{x:.2f}" for x in row))
    else:
        print("No reduced data (all features are constant)")
    
    # Step 4: Reconstruct data
    try:
        X_reconstructed = reducer.reconstruct(X_reduced)
    except Exception as e:
        print("Error during reconstruction:", e)
        return
    
    print("\nReconstructed data:")
    print(X_reconstructed)
    
    # Step 5: Evaluate reconstruction error
    try:
        error = reducer.evaluate_error(A, X_reconstructed)
    except Exception as e:
        print("Error during error evaluation:", e)
        return
    
    print(f"\nReconstruction error: {error:.2f}")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()

09/09/25, 13:55 - Sumit Nair: CREATE TABLE Fest (
    Fest_ID VARCHAR(10) PRIMARY KEY,
    Fest_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    Year INT NOT NULL,
    Head_teamID VARCHAR(10)
);

CREATE TABLE Teams (
    Team_ID VARCHAR(10) PRIMARY KEY,
    Team_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    Team_Type VARCHAR(3),
    FestID VARCHAR(10),
    FOREIGN KEY (FestID) REFERENCES Fest(Fest_ID)
);

CREATE TABLE Members (
    Mem_ID VARCHAR(10) PRIMARY KEY,
    Mem_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    DOB DATE,
    super_MemID VARCHAR(10),
    TeamID VARCHAR(10),
    FOREIGN KEY (TeamID) REFERENCES Teams(Team_ID),
    FOREIGN KEY (super_MemID) REFERENCES Members(Mem_ID)
);

CREATE TABLE Event (
    Event_ID VARCHAR(10) PRIMARY KEY,
    Event_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    Building VARCHAR(50),
    Floor INT,
    Room_no VARCHAR(10),
    Price DECIMAL(10, 2),
    TeamID VARCHAR(10),
    FOREIGN KEY (TeamID) REFERENCES Teams(Team_ID)
);

CREATE TABLE Event_conduction (
    Event_ID VARCHAR(10),
    Date_of_conduction DATE,
    PRIMARY KEY (Event_ID, Date_of_conduction),
    FOREIGN KEY (Event_ID) REFERENCES Event(Event_ID)
);

CREATE TABLE Participants (
    SRN VARCHAR(20) PRIMARY KEY,
    name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    department VARCHAR(100),
    semester INT,
    gender VARCHAR(10)
);

CREATE TABLE Visitors (
    SRN VARCHAR(20),
    Name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    age INT,
    gender VARCHAR(10),
    PRIMARY KEY (SRN, Name),
    FOREIGN KEY (SRN) REFERENCES Participants(SRN)
);

CREATE TABLE Registration (
    RegistrationID VARCHAR(20) PRIMARY KEY,
    Event_ID VARCHAR(10),
    SRN VARCHAR(20),
    FOREIGN KEY (Event_ID) REFERENCES Event(Event_ID),
    FOREIGN KEY (SRN) REFERENCES Participants(SRN)
);

CREATE TABLE Stall (
    Stall_ID VARCHAR(10) PRIMARY KEY,
    name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    Fest_ID VARCHAR(10),
    FOREIGN KEY (Fest_ID) REFERENCES Fest(Fest_ID)
);

CREATE TABLE Item (
    Name VARCHAR(100) PRIMARY KEY,
    Type VARCHAR(10)
);

CREATE TABLE Stall_items (
    Stall_ID VARCHAR(10),
    Item_name VARCHAR(100),
    Price_per_unit DECIMAL(10, 2),
    Total_quantity INT,
    PRIMARY KEY (Stall_ID, Item_name),
    FOREIGN KEY (Stall_ID) REFERENCES Stall(Stall_ID),
    FOREIGN KEY (Item_name) REFERENCES Item(Name)
);

CREATE TABLE Purchased (
    Purchase_ID INT PRIMARY KEY,
    SRN VARCHAR(20),
    Stall_ID VARCHAR(10),
    Item_name VARCHAR(100),
    Timestamp DATETIME,
    Quantity INT,
    FOREIGN KEY (SRN) REFERENCES Participants(SRN),
    FOREIGN KEY (Stall_ID) REFERENCES Stall(Stall_ID),
    FOREIGN KEY (Item_name) REFERENCES Item(Name)
);

26/09/25, 21:03 - Sohil Nigga (Drug Dealer Who Can't Eat Drugs): Great question üëç You‚Äôre asking in the context of a Social Computing (SC) project ‚Äì ‚Äúwhich part of J.A.R.V.I.S. (Iron Man‚Äôs AI assistant) could realistically be built as a mid-complexity project within half a semester.‚Äù

Let‚Äôs break it down:


---

üîπ What J.A.R.V.I.S. is (in SC terms)

J.A.R.V.I.S. is a mix of:

Speech interface (NLP, voice commands)

Information retrieval (fetching updates, summarizing)

Social interaction (acting as a conversational agent)

Task delegation (managing tasks, reminders, control of devices)

Decision support (suggestions, recommendations)


A full J.A.R.V.I.S. is sci-fi, but for a Social Computing project, you only need to implement one or two realistic modules that align with SC concepts like interaction, collaboration, recommendation, sentiment analysis, or collective intelligence.


---

üîπ J.A.R.V.I.S. Features You Could Build (Mid Complexity, Half-Semester)

1. Personalized News & Sentiment Analyzer

What it does: Takes user‚Äôs voice/text input (‚ÄúGive me today‚Äôs news on climate change‚Äù) ‚Üí fetches news/Twitter posts ‚Üí summarizes + shows sentiment (positive/negative).

SC connection: Uses crowdsourced data (social media) to provide insights.

Tools: Python (News API, Tweepy), VADER/TextBlob for sentiment, simple web/voice UI.



---

2. Smart Social Task Assistant

What it does: Lets a group of users add tasks/events ‚Üí system organizes and suggests best times/priority ‚Üí group can vote/like tasks.

SC connection: Collaborative task management (crowdsourcing).

Tools: Flask/Django + SQLite + simple voting mechanism.



---

3. Mini Social Recommender (‚ÄúWhat should I watch/listen to?‚Äù)

What it does: Users ask J.A.R.V.I.S. for a recommendation ‚Üí system aggregates preferences from peers (social filtering) ‚Üí recommends movies, songs, or courses.

SC connection: Collective intelligence & recommendation systems.

Tools: Flask + collaborative filtering (Surprise library) + basic UI.



---

4. Social Media Summarizer

What it does: User says ‚ÄúJ.A.R.V.I.S., summarize what people are saying about cricket today.‚Äù ‚Üí System fetches trending tweets/posts ‚Üí summarizes main points.

SC connection: Uses large-scale user-generated content and condenses it for personal use.

Tools: Twitter API, Hugging Face summarizer, dashboard UI.



---

5. Toxic Comment Moderator (Jarvis for Online Communities)

What it does: Works as a forum/chat moderator. J.A.R.V.I.S. flags toxic or off-topic comments and highlights useful ones.

SC connection: Community moderation & social interaction quality.

Tools: Perspective API / HuggingFace toxicity model + chat/forum backend.



---

üîπ Recommended Pick for You

Since you want a mid-level, doable SC project in half a semester ‚Üí the most practical J.A.R.V.I.S.-like parts are:

‚úÖ Social Media Summarizer (collect + analyze + present)
‚úÖ Mini Social Recommender (recommendations using group preferences)
‚úÖ Toxic Comment Moderator (AI-powered moderator for communities)

All 3 are socially-driven, computationally feasible, and aligned with SC.


---

29/09/25, 11:39 - Sohil Nigga (Drug Dealer Who Can't Eat Drugs): Project Idea in One Line

‚ÄúJarvis-like assistant for student teams that can fetch deadlines, track contributions, and summarize group chats.‚Äù


---

üîπ Core Features (Minimum Viable Product)

1. Deadline Fetcher

Stores deadlines in a shared Google Sheet/Database.

User asks: ‚ÄúJarvis, what‚Äôs the deadline for CN project?‚Äù

Jarvis fetches & responds (text/voice).



2. Contribution Tracker

Each member updates status (e.g., uploaded file link / progress %).

User asks: ‚ÄúWho has submitted their part?‚Äù

Jarvis replies with progress summary.



3. Chat Summarizer

Connect to a WhatsApp/Telegram group (via bot).

Summarizes the last N messages into key points.





---

üîπ Optional ‚ÄúCool‚Äù Add-ons (if time permits)

Voice Commands ‚Üí Speech-to-text + Jarvis-style TTS replies.

Smart Notifications ‚Üí ‚ÄúReminder: only 2 days left for CN project submission.‚Äù

Sentiment Monitor ‚Üí Detect tone of group discussions (positive, stressed, off-topic).



---

üîπ Tools & Technologies

Backend: Python (Flask/Django)

Data Sources:

Google Sheets API (deadlines)

Telegram Bot API (group chat)


NLP: HuggingFace (summarization) or simple TextRank summarizer

Database: SQLite (for team contributions)

UI Options:

Telegram Bot UI (simplest)

OR Web dashboard (for visualization)


Voice I/O (optional): SpeechRecognition + pyttsx3



---

üîπ Workflow (How It Will Work)

1. User Input (via Telegram bot or web app):

‚ÄúJarvis, when is the CN deadline?‚Äù

‚ÄúSummarize yesterday‚Äôs group chat.‚Äù

‚ÄúShow progress of submissions.‚Äù



2. Processing:

Deadline requests ‚Üí Query Google Sheet.

Contribution ‚Üí Read DB of member updates.

Chat summary ‚Üí Fetch last N messages ‚Üí Apply NLP summarization.



3. Output:

Text reply in chat.

Optional: Voice reply (‚ÄúSir, CN project deadline is on March 16.‚Äù).





---

üîπ Deliverables

Working prototype (Telegram bot/web app)

Demo: Show asking Jarvis about deadlines, contributions, and chat summaries.

Report: Explain how it uses social computing (collaboration, collective intelligence, group interaction support).



---

üîπ Suggested Timeline (6‚Äì8 Weeks)

Week 1: Finalize features, set up repo, pick APIs/tools.

Week 2‚Äì3: Implement deadline fetcher (Google Sheets) + contribution tracker (DB).

Week 4: Add chat summarization via Telegram bot.

Week 5: Integrate all features into one assistant.

Week 6: Polish UI, add optional voice commands.

Week 7: Testing with your class/team.

Week 8: Prepare demo & documentation.



---

29/09/25, 12:07 - Sohil Nigga (Drug Dealer Who Can't Eat Drugs): Structure for Your Initial Presentation (Jarvis-WhatsApp Project)
1. Title Slide

Title: ‚ÄúJarvis: An NLP-based Assistant for WhatsApp Chat Summarization and Task Management‚Äù

Team Members: [Your Names]

Course: Social Computing

2. Introduction / Motivation

WhatsApp is the most widely used collaboration tool.

Problem: Long chats ‚Üí information overload.

Users struggle to track decisions, deadlines, and contributions.

Industry baseline: WhatsApp just launched ‚Äúmessage summaries‚Äù (2025) but only for private messages.

Gap: No effective team-oriented summarizer for group chats (with tasks/reminders).

üëâ End with: ‚ÄúOur goal is to design a task-aware summarization assistant (‚ÄòJarvis‚Äô) for WhatsApp groups.‚Äù

3. Base Paper (Literature Background)

Example base paper: Lu et al., 2025 ‚Äî MRDS for Few-shot Dialogue Summarization

Uses LLMs to synthesize training data ‚Üí improves summarization when data is scarce.

Other related works: Hierarchical attention (Yang et al., 2025), Graph-based task extraction, WhatsApp chat summarizer prototype (2025).

Gap identified: None of these focus on WhatsApp group-task awareness.

4. Problem Statement

‚ÄúWe aim to extend dialogue summarization techniques for WhatsApp group chats by focusing on task-aware summaries that highlight deadlines, action items, and contributions. Our approach adapts [base paper method] to WhatsApp-style chats using publicly available datasets (SAMSum, DialogSum) and small-scale WhatsApp exports.‚Äù

5. Objectives

Summarize WhatsApp group chats into concise overviews.

Extract tasks, decisions, and deadlines.

Provide chat-based reminders.

Evaluate performance on standard datasets (SAMSum, DialogSum) + WhatsApp case study.

6. Methodology (High-Level Plan)

Dataset: Start with SAMSum, extend with small WhatsApp exports.

Model: Fine-tune summarization model (BART/T5) or apply MRDS (LLM augmentation).

Extension: Add task/decision extraction ‚Üí convert into bullet points.

Integration: Deploy prototype that can send summaries back into WhatsApp (using Twilio or yowsup API).

7. Feasibility

Datasets are openly available.

Pre-trained models (BART/T5/PEGASUS) ready on HuggingFace.

Fine-tuning is possible on lab GPUs in 1‚Äì2 days.

Timeline: Half-semester ‚Üí feasible scope.

8. Expected Outcomes

A WhatsApp group chat assistant (‚ÄúJarvis‚Äù) that:

Summarizes daily chats.

Extracts tasks/deadlines.

Provides reminders inside WhatsApp.

Evaluation: ROUGE/BERTScore on SAMSum + human evaluation on WhatsApp.

9. Timeline (Gantt chart style if possible)

Week 1‚Äì2: Literature Review & Dataset setup

Week 3‚Äì4: Model fine-tuning / LLM prompting

Week 5: Add task extraction module

Week 6: WhatsApp integration prototype

Week 7: Testing & Evaluation

Week 8: Report & Presentation

10. Conclusion

Project bridges NLP + Social Computing.

Novelty: Task-aware summarization for WhatsApp (team setting).

Feasible within semester, aligns with NLP + SC project guidelines

14/10/25, 00:10 - Sohil Nigga (Drug Dealer Who Can't Eat Drugs): Project Report: Neighborhood and Price Prediction for Albany Airbnb Listings
Author: [SOHIL N S and SUMIT SANTOSH NAIR]

 Date: October 14, 2025

Abstract
This project explores the predictability of Airbnb listing characteristics in Albany, NY, using a dataset from "Inside Airbnb." We developed two machine learning models to address distinct classification tasks: predicting a listing's neighborhood and predicting whether its price is above or below the local median. The methodology involved extensive feature engineering, including TF-IDF vectorization with n-grams for text data and one-hot encoding for structured data. A Linear Support Vector Classifier (LinearSVC) was trained for both tasks, with hyperparameter tuning performed via GridSearchCV. The neighborhood prediction model achieved high performance, with 86.7% accuracy and 90.5% precision, indicating that textual descriptions contain strong geographic signals. The price prediction model, however, showed clear signs of overfitting, performing well on the development set but achieving only 66.7% accuracy on the test set. This report details our data processing pipeline, feature engineering strategy, modeling results, and provides a discussion on model performance and future work, including strategies to mitigate overfitting.

1. Introduction
The online rental marketplace, exemplified by platforms like Airbnb, has become a significant component of the hospitality industry. The vast amount of data generated by these platforms presents an opportunity to model and understand the factors that define a listing's market position and geographic identity.

This project focuses on the Airbnb market in Albany, New York. Our primary goal is to determine the extent to which machine learning models can classify listings based on publicly available data. We address two specific research questions:

Neighborhood Prediction: Can a listing's neighborhood be accurately predicted using the contents of its description and its structured features (e.g., room type, amenities)? A successful model would highlight the unique linguistic and feature-based signatures of different neighborhoods.

Price Range Prediction: Can we predict whether a listing's price is above or below the median price for the area? This binary classification task is a proxy for understanding what features are associated with higher-value properties.

This report outlines our methodology, from data preparation and feature engineering to model training and evaluation. We present a detailed analysis of the results for both models and conclude with a discussion of our findings and potential avenues for future research.

2. Methodology
Our approach involved a multi-stage pipeline designed to transform raw listing data into a format suitable for machine learning and to train robust classification models.

2.1. Data
The dataset was sourced from the "Inside Airbnb" project, providing a comprehensive snapshot of listings in Albany. Key fields utilized in this study included:

Textual Data: summary, space, description, neighborhood_overview, transit, etc.

Categorical Data: property_type, room_type, cancellation_policy.

Numerical Data: price, bedrooms, bathrooms, accommodates, cleaning_fee.

2.2. Feature Engineering
To capture the varied information within the dataset, we engineered three distinct sets of features, which were later combined.

TF-IDF Features: The textual data from all relevant fields was concatenated. We used TfidfVectorizer to convert this text into a numerical format. Key parameters included:

N-grams: ngram_range=(1, 2) was used to capture both individual words and two-word phrases, which can provide more context (e.g., "downtown albany").

Preprocessing: A custom analyzer was used to perform stemming, remove stop words, and filter out neighborhood names to prevent data leakage.

Feature Pruning: We set max_features=2000 and min_df=3 to keep the feature space manageable and remove very rare terms.

Handpicked Text Features: We computed sentiment scores (polarity, subjectivity) and word/character counts from the descriptions.

Structured Features: Categorical features were one-hot encoded, and numerical features were used directly.

2.3. Modeling Pipeline
The modeling process was standardized for both prediction tasks:

Data Splitting: The dataset was split into training (80%), development (10%), and testing (10%) sets using a stratified approach to maintain class distribution.

Feature Scaling: All combined features were scaled using MaxAbsScaler, which is essential for the performance of SVMs and is well-suited for sparse matrices.

Feature Selection: We employed Recursive Feature Elimination (RFE) to select the most informative features for each model.

Model Selection: A Linear Support Vector Classifier (LinearSVC) was chosen due to its high efficiency with high-dimensional, sparse data common in text classification. class_weight="balanced" was used to handle any class imbalance.

Hyperparameter Tuning: GridSearchCV with 3-fold stratified cross-validation was used to find the optimal regularization hyperparameter C for the SVM.

3. Results
The trained models were evaluated on the held-out test set. The results for each task are presented below.

3.1. Neighborhood Prediction Model
This model demonstrated high predictive power, successfully identifying the correct neighborhood for the vast majority of listings.

Test Accuracy: 86.7%

Test Precision: 90.5%

Test F1-Score: 87.0%

Best C Hyperparameter: 1000

The high precision indicates that when the model predicted a certain neighborhood, it was very likely to be correct.

3.2. Price Prediction Model
The price prediction model showed a significant discrepancy between its performance on the development and test sets, a clear indicator of overfitting.

Development Accuracy: 85.7%

Test Accuracy: 66.7%

Test Precision: 67.3%

Test F1-Score: 66.7%

Best C Hyperparameter: 1000

The model learned patterns specific to the training/development data that did not generalize to the unseen test data.

4. Discussion
The results provide valuable insights into the Albany Airbnb market and our modeling approach.

The outstanding performance of the neighborhood prediction model confirms that neighborhoods in Albany have distinct "linguistic fingerprints." Phrases related to local landmarks, street names, and ambiance captured by the TF-IDF and n-gram features are powerful predictors of location.

In contrast, the price prediction model suffered from overfitting. The selection of a very large C value (1000) by GridSearchCV indicates that the model was too complex. A large C reduces the strength of regularization, encouraging the model to fit the training data as closely as possible, including its noise. This resulted in a model that memorized the training set rather than learning generalizable patterns about what makes a listing expensive.

5. Conclusion and Future Work
This project successfully developed a high-accuracy model for predicting Airbnb neighborhoods in Albany, demonstrating the value of text-based features. We also identified a clear case of overfitting in our price prediction model, which provides a critical learning for future iterations.

Based on these findings, we recommend the following next steps:

Address Overfitting in Price Model: The most critical next step is to retrain the price model with stronger regularization. This can be achieved by providing GridSearchCV with a range of smaller C values (e.g., [0.01, 0.1, 1, 10]), forcing it to choose a simpler, more generalizable model.

Feature Importance Analysis: For the successful neighborhood model, an analysis of feature importance should be conducted to identify which specific words and phrases are the strongest indicators for each neighborhood.
